## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: chemical kinetics
**Analysis**: The target dA_dt represents the rate of concentration change in a chemical reaction, a classic chemical kinetics problem where the rate law typically depends on the current concentration A and occasionally on time t through time-dependent rate parameters.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Zero-order kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 (constant reaction rate, independent of A)
- First-order kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A (exponential decay of A)
- Second-order kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A**2 (hyperbolic decay of A)
- Fractional-order kinetics: dA_dt = -c0 * A**c1 (power-law dependence, complex mechanism)
- Mixed first- and second-order: dA_dt = -c0 * A - c1 * A**2 (parallel or consecutive reaction pathways)
- Autocatalytic logistic rate: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A) (self-catalyzed growth with saturation)
- Reversible first-order: dA_dt = -c0 * A + c1 * (c2 - A) (forward and backward reactions, equilibrium)
- Time-dependent rate (exponential decay): dA_dt = -c0 * exp(-c1 * t) * A (loss of catalytic activity or reagent)
- Time-dependent rate (power-law decay): dA_dt = -c0 * (1 + c1 * t)**(-c2) * A (progressive deactivation)
- Shifted power-law: dA_dt = -c0 * (A + c1)**c2 (threshold or offset concentration before reaction)
- Rate with hyperbolic inhibition: dA_dt = -c0 * A / (1 + c1 * A + c2 * A**2) (substrate inhibition or complex surface kinetics)
- Exponential dependence on A: dA_dt = -c0 * exp(-c1 * A) * A (empirical rate for certain catalytic systems)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Constant rate (dA_dt independent of A) suggests zero-order kinetics, often when a catalyst is saturated or reactant is in excess.
- Linear dependence on A (dA_dt ∝ A) indicates first-order kinetics, typical for unimolecular reactions.
- Quadratic dependence (dA_dt ∝ A**2) points to second-order kinetics, common in bimolecular elementary steps.
- Power-law dependence A**c with non‑integer exponent c suggests a fractional reaction order, often arising from complex reaction networks.
- Explicit time dependence like exp(-c1*t) in the rate law implies a time‑varying rate constant, e.g., catalyst deactivation or changing medium conditions.
- A logistic‑type product A*(c1 - A) describes autocatalytic reactions where the product accelerates its own formation until saturation.
- A difference between forward and reverse rate terms (e.g., -c0*A + c1*(c2-A)) models reversible reactions approaching equilibrium.
- Rational forms like 1/(1 + c1*A) in the denominator reflect saturation or inhibition effects, as seen in surface‑catalyzed or enzyme‑mimicking processes.
- Additive rate terms (e.g., sum of first‑ and second‑order) indicate multiple parallel reaction channels contributing to the overall consumption of A.
